9:[["$","meta","0",{"name":"viewport","content":"width=device-width, initial-scale=1"}],["$","meta","1",{"charSet":"utf-8"}],["$","title","2",{"children":"D10f | Configuration Management With Node.js"}],["$","meta","3",{"name":"description","content":"Handle configuration of your application through environment variables, including validation and enforcing a schema."}],["$","meta","4",{"name":"keywords","content":"nodejs typescript configuration server application deployment, nodejs,typescript,docker"}]]

a:I[3475,["750","static/chunks/750-0f816d740819c251.js","308","static/chunks/app/blog/%5Bslug%5D/page-920a7887c59b1504.js"],"default"]
b:T3f37f,<div id="preamble">
<div class="sectionbody">
<div class="paragraph">
<p>Configuration and code should live in strict isolation from each other. Everything that is likely to change between deployments, is configuration: database credentials, API keys, hostnames, and even global constants used in code. This model scales up smoothly as the app naturally expands in size and complexity, and by extension, the number of environments it will be deployed on.</p>
</div>
</div>
</div><menu class="toc"><li class="toc__item"><a href="#_environment_variables">Environment Variables</a></li><li class="toc__item"><a href="#_schema_validation">Schema Validation</a><ul><li class="toc__item"><a href="#_create_a_configuration_service_class">Create A Configuration Service Class</a></li></ul></li><li class="toc__item"><a href="#_handling_sensitive_information">Handling Sensitive Information</a><ul><li class="toc__item"><a href="#_docker_swarm_secrets">Docker Swarm Secrets</a></li></ul></li></menu>
<div class="sect1"><h2 id="_environment_variables">Environment Variables<a class="anchor" href="#_environment_variables">
                    <svg aria-hidden="true">
                        <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-link" />
                    </svg>
                </a></h2>
<div class="sectionbody">
<div class="paragraph">
<p>In Node.js, the global object <code>process.env</code> contains information related to the execution environment; the properties found in it, are environment variables. They may contain information such as the username who owns the process, the current location of the entry point, etc. However, we can add our own variables to this in order to customize the behavior of our application. For example, we could specify the size limit of file upload:</p>
</div>

            <button class="codeblock__copy-btn rotating-btn  js-required">

                    </svg>

                    <svg aria-hidden="true" style="transform: rotate(180deg);">
                        <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-clipboard-success" />
                    </svg>
                </span>
            </button>

                </figure>
            </div>
        </div>
<div class="paragraph">
<p>To make a variable available to the Node.js process we declare it at the command line, using the <code>export</code> keyword; this would make the variable last throughout the shell session. We can also declare them at the same time as we run some process, and they would exist for that particular process but cease to exist immediately otherwise. This is sometimes used within <code>package.json</code> files, in the scripts section:</p>
</div>
<div class="codeblock">

                    </svg>

                    <svg aria-hidden="true" style="transform: rotate(180deg);">
                        <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-clipboard-success" />
                    </svg>
                </span>
            </button>

        </div>
<div class="paragraph">
<p>However, a far more common approach for providing environment variables to our application is through an external file (typically called <code>.env</code>). This it far more portable between developers and environments, including deployments, and much easier to maintain over time.</p>
</div>
<div class="codeblock">
            <button class="codeblock__copy-btn rotating-btn mt-6 js-required">
                <span class="unselectable">
                    <svg aria-hidden="true">
                        <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-clipboard" />
                    </svg>

                    <svg aria-hidden="true" style="transform: rotate(180deg);">
                        <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-clipboard-success" />
                    </svg>
                </span>
            </button>
            <div class="listingblock" data-language="javascript">
                <span style="font-style: italic;">.env</span>
                <figure>
                    <figcaption class="visually-hidden">.env</figcaption>
                    <pre class="shiki shiki-themes catppuccin-macchiato catppuccin-latte catppuccin-frappe everforest-dark everforest-light solarized-light solarized-dark gruvbox-light-hard class="conum pl-2" data-value="1"><b>❶</b></i></span></span></code></pre>
                </figure>
            </div>
        </div>
<div class="colist arabic">
                <table>
                    <tbody>
                        <tr>
            <td>
                <i class="conum pr-2" data-value="1"></i>
                <b>❶</b>
            </td>
            <td>Write each environment variable in its own line, without spaces.</td>
        </tr>
                    </tbody>
                </table>
            </div>
<div class="paragraph">
<p>For a very long time, a popular package called <code>dotenv</code> has been used to read from this file automatically when the application starts. This saves us time in writing boilerplate logic about it:</p>

            </div>
        </div>
<div class="colist arabic">
                <table>
                    <tbody>
                        <tr>
            <td>
                <i class="conum pr-2" data-value="1"></i>
                <b>❶</b>
            </td>
            <td>Start the application passing the <code>--env-file</code> flag pointing to the file in question.</td>
        </tr>
                    </tbody>
                </table>
            </div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="sect1"><h2 id="_schema_validation">Schema Validation<a class="anchor" href="#_schema_validation">
                    <svg aria-hidden="true">
                        <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-link" />
                    </svg>
                </a></h2>
<div class="sectionbody">
<div class="paragraph">
<p>Environment variables can be considered a form of user input and as such, we want to validate them. This can help to catch errors early and use sane defaults when certain variables are not provided. In addition, one important property of environment variables is that they are always strings and may need to be explicitly cast into their correct type when used inside our application.</p>
</div>
<div class="paragraph">
<p>There are quite a few packages that can be used for this, but <a href="https://zod.dev/">zod</a> is one that has gained a lot of traction in the past few years.</p>
</div>

            <div class="admonition admonition--left note">
                <svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg">
                    <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-note" />
                </svg>
                <p>I will give a shout out to joi as an honorable mention as well. Is still in active development, but I haven't used it in quite a while as I found it lacking in some areas regarding TypeScript support (this may have changed since last time I checked it out).</p>
            </div>
<div class="paragraph">
<p>We can create a file that will contain our schema definition, describing the properties that should exist in our application and constraints about them. For example, it wouldn&#8217;t make sense to have a negative value as the maximum file size to upload (unless for some reason you decide that signifies something special).</p>
</div>
<div class="codeblock">
            <button class="codeblock__copy-btn rotating-btn mt-6 js-required">
                <span class="unselectable">
                    <svg aria-hidden="true">
                        <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-clipboard" />
                    </svg>

                    <svg aria-hidden="true" style="transform: rotate(180deg);">
                        <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-clipboard-success" />
                    </svg>
                </span>
            </button>

        </div>
<div class="colist arabic">
                <table>
                    <tbody>
                        <tr>
            <td>
                <i class="conum pr-2" data-value="1"></i>
                <b>❶</b>
            </td>
            <td>We validate that the property exists and is a string, and has a default value of '1024' if is not specified. We also transform it using a function to cast it into a number.</td>
        </tr><tr>
            <td>
                <i class="conum pr-2" data-value="2"></i>
                <b>❷</b>
            </td>
            <td>The type of the schema object before the values are transformed.</td>
        </tr><tr>
            <td>
                <i class="conum pr-2" data-value="3"></i>
                <b>❸</b>
            </td>
            <td>The type of the resulting schema object after transformation. This is what our application should use.</td>
        </tr>
                    </tbody>
                </table>
            </div>
<div class="paragraph">
<p>This library is quite handy for two reasons: 1) it allows us to infer the types very easily 2) we can use both the type of the object before and after it&#8217;s processed. We can take advantage of the latter to validate the properties from the environment as they were provided in order to detect errors, as we mentioned.</p>
</div>
<div class="sect2"><h3 id="_create_a_configuration_service_class">Create A Configuration Service Class<a class="anchor" href="#_create_a_configuration_service_class">
                    <svg aria-hidden="true">
                        <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-link" />
                    </svg>
                </a></h3>
<div class="paragraph">
<p>To keep things organized we can create a service to encapsulate all the logic pertaining to validating and, later, accessing the configuration throughout our application.</p>
</div>

            </div>
        </div>
<div class="colist arabic">
                <table>
                    <tbody>
                        <tr>
            <td>
                <i class="conum pr-2" data-value="1"></i>
                <b>❶</b>
            </td>
            <td>The final configuration for our application, validated and transformed into the appropriate values and types.</td>
        </tr><tr>
            <td>
                <i class="conum pr-2" data-value="2"></i>
                <b>❷</b>
            </td>
            <td>Receive the schema object generated by the zod library.</td>
        </tr><tr>
            <td>
                <i class="conum pr-2" data-value="3"></i>
                <b>❸</b>
            </td>
            <td>Read environment variables, somehow. This will be more important in a bit.</td>
        </tr><tr>
            <td>
                <i class="conum pr-2" data-value="4"></i>
                <b>❹</b>
            </td>
            <td>Here we validate the whole object with our environment variables. Most libraries will prune everything that is not part of the schema.</td>
        </tr>
                    </tbody>
                </table>
            </div>
<div class="paragraph">
<p>We can now use our configuration class to access the validated environment variables, enjoying the full benefit of type inference and autocompletion in our code:</p>

            <div class="admonition admonition--right important">
                <svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg">
                    <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-important" />
                </svg>
                <p>There are many improvements we can make to this class. For example, it's going to be used throughout our entire codebase, so it would be better to design it as a singleton, or implement some form of caching, so that we don't validate the same object over and over again at runtime.</p>
            </div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="sect1"><h2 id="_handling_sensitive_information">Handling Sensitive Information<a class="anchor" href="#_handling_sensitive_information">
                    <svg aria-hidden="true">
                        <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-link" />
                    </svg>
                </a></h2>
<div class="sectionbody">
<div class="paragraph">
<p>Quite often, environment variables deal with sensitive information that you wouldn&#8217;t necessarily want to expose to the public. This is why <code>.env</code> files are ignored by version control systems by default. It makes it more difficult to deploy applications without exposing those secrets; indeed, one of the biggest challenges regarding security is dealing with keys and secrets securely.</p>
</div>
<div class="paragraph">
<p>This is where Secret Management services come in, such as Vault by Hashicorp. However, to keep things simple I&#8217;ll use as an example a much more rudimentary and often overlooked technology that handles this aspect of environment variables quite nicely: Docker Swarm.</p>
</div>
<div class="sect2"><h3 id="_docker_swarm_secrets">Docker Swarm Secrets<a class="anchor" href="#_docker_swarm_secrets">
                    <svg aria-hidden="true">
                        <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-link" />
                    </svg>
                </a></h3>
<div class="paragraph">
<p>Docker Secrets is a feature that allows a Docker Swarm Manager node to hold sensitive information encrypted at rest, and pass it to any running services that need it. There are many container images that accept environment variables in form of a <em>string</em> or a <em>path to a file</em>, by using a "_FILE" suffix. This makes it very convenient to use the same compose file for production and still commit it to version control without exposing any sensitive information.</p>
</div>
<div class="paragraph">
<p>Here&#8217;s an example of how that would look like, using our <code>MAX_FILE_SIZE</code> variable we&#8217;ve seen thus far, along with a Mongo database also reading values from the environment in the form of secrets:</p>
</div>

            <button class="codeblock__copy-btn rotating-btn mt-6 js-required">

                    <svg aria-hidden="true">
                        <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-clipboard" />
                    </svg>

                    <svg aria-hidden="true" style="transform: rotate(180deg);">
                        <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-clipboard-success" />
                    </svg>
                </span>
            </button>

        </div>

            <div class="admonition admonition--left note">
                <svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg">
                    <use href="/sprite.svg#icon-note" />
                </svg>
                <p>You could also suffix your variables like this inside your .env file, but with Docker Swarm in particular this would probably be the most common way to do it.</p>
            </div>
<div class="paragraph">
<p>Based on this compose file we don&#8217;t know what the secrets are, but we know they are made available and exist only within this container instance. We can now update our configuration service class to read from these locations</p>
</div>

<span class="line"><span style="color:#939AB7;--shiki-catppuccin-light:#7C7F93;--shiki-catppuccin-dark:#949CBB;--shiki-everforest-dark:#D3C6AA;--shiki-everforest-light:#5C6A72;--shiki-solarized-light:#657B83;--shiki-solarized-dark:#839496;--shiki-gruvbox-light:#7C6F64;--shiki-gruvbox-dark:#A89984">}</span></span></code></pre>
                </figure>

        </div>
<div class="colist arabic">
                <table>
                    <tbody>
                        <tr>
            <td>
                <i class="conum pr-2" data-value="1"></i>
                <b>❶</b>
            </td>
            <td>Ignore variables that do not end with "_FILE".</td>
        </tr><tr>
            <td>
                <i class="conum pr-2" data-value="2"></i>
                <b>❷</b>
            </td>
            <td>Remove newline character added by Node's <code>readFileSync</code>.</td>
        </tr><tr>
            <td>
                <i class="conum pr-2" data-value="3"></i>
                <b>❸</b>
            </td>
            <td>You could choose to be a little more loose with this and use a default value. Although, this would be a good way to detect errors with deployment setups as well.</td>
        </tr>
                    </tbody>
                </table>
            </div>
<div class="paragraph">
<p>Since this happens <em>before</em> the schema validation all environment variables are processed in the right order. Mind you, this process would work all the same even when not deploying through Docker, but we have made our application more resilient to changes in the environment in which it is deployed.</p>
</div>
<div class="paragraph">
<p>And that&#8217;s it! From this point on is just a matter of playing with the schema and use this class everywhere you need an environment variable in your code. There are a number of improvements that this class needs to be production-ready, but this should handle most cases just fine.</p>
</div>
